      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       This article is about the historical city from 330 to 1453. For its predecessor in Greek and early Roman times, see
       <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">
        Byzantium
       </a>
       . For Constantinople during the Ottoman era and the modern city, see
       <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">
        Istanbul
       </a>
       . For other uses, see
       <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Constantinople_(disambiguation)" title="Constantinople (disambiguation)">
        Constantinople (disambiguation)
       </a>
       .
      </div>

      <p>
       <b>
        Constantinople
       </b>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">
        Greek
       </a>
       :
       <span lang="el" xml:lang="el">
        Κωνσταντινούπολις
       </span>
       <i>
        Konstantinoúpolis
       </i>
       ;
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">
        Latin
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        <span lang="la" xml:lang="la">
         Constantinopolis
        </span>
       </i>
       ) was the
       <a href="/wiki/Capital_city" title="Capital city">
        capital city
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">
        Roman
       </a>
       /
       <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">
        Byzantine
       </a>
       (330–1204 and 1261–1453), and also of the brief
       <a href="/wiki/Latin_Empire" title="Latin Empire">
        Latin
       </a>
       (1204–1261), and the later
       <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">
        Ottoman
       </a>
       (1453–1923) empires. It was reinaugurated in 324 AD from ancient
       <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">
        Byzantium
       </a>
       as the new capital of the Roman Empire by Emperor
       <a href="/wiki/Constantine_the_Great" title="Constantine the Great">
        Constantine the Great
       </a>
       , after whom it was named, and dedicated on 11 May 330 AD.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODB_5-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ODB-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       From the mid-5th century to the early 13th century, Constantinople was the largest and wealthiest city in Europe
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and it was instrumental in the advancement of Christianity during Roman and Byzantine times as the home of the
       <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Patriarch_of_Constantinople" title="Ecumenical Patriarch of Constantinople">
        Ecumenical Patriarch of Constantinople
       </a>
       and as the guardian of Christendom's holiest relics such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_thorns" title="Crown of thorns">
        Crown of Thorns
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/True_Cross" title="True Cross">
        True Cross
       </a>
       . After the final loss of its provinces in the early 15th century, the Byzantine Empire was reduced to just Constantinople and its environs, along with
       <a href="/wiki/Morea" title="Morea">
        Morea
       </a>
       in Greece, and the city
       <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">
        eventually fell
       </a>
       to the Ottomans after a 53-day siege on 29 May 1453.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bizansist_touchup.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="970" data-file-width="2516" height="116" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Bizansist_touchup.jpg/300px-Bizansist_touchup.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Bizansist_touchup.jpg/450px-Bizansist_touchup.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Bizansist_touchup.jpg/600px-Bizansist_touchup.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bizansist_touchup.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Aerial view of Byzantine Constantinople and the Propontis (Sea of Marmara).
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Constantinople was famed for its massive and complex defences. Although besieged on numerous occasions by various peoples, the defences of Constantinople proved invulnerable for nearly nine hundred years before the city was taken in 1204 by the Crusader armies of the
       <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Crusade" title="Fourth Crusade">
        Fourth Crusade
       </a>
       , and after it was liberated in 1261 by the Byzantine Emperor
       <a href="/wiki/Michael_VIII_Palaiologos" title="Michael VIII Palaiologos">
        Michael VIII Palaiologos
       </a>
       , a second and final time in 1453 when it was conquered by the Ottoman
       <a href="/wiki/Sultan" title="Sultan">
        Sultan
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mehmed_II" title="Mehmed II">
        Mehmed II
       </a>
       . The first wall of the city was erected by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantine_I" title="Constantine I">
        Constantine I
       </a>
       , and surrounded the city on both land and sea fronts. Later, in the 5th century, the
       <a href="/wiki/Praetorian_prefect" title="Praetorian prefect">
        Praetorian Prefect
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Anthemius_(praetorian_prefect)" title="Anthemius (praetorian prefect)">
        Anthemius
       </a>
       under the child emperor
       <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_II" title="Theodosius II">
        Theodosius II
       </a>
       undertook the construction of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theodosian_Walls" title="Theodosian Walls">
        Theodosian Walls
       </a>
       , which consisted of a double wall lying about 2 km (1.2 miles) to the west of the first wall and a moat with palisades in front.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This formidable complex of defences was one of the most sophisticated of
       <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">
        Antiquity
       </a>
       . The city was built intentionally to rival Rome, and it was claimed that several elevations within its walls matched the 'seven hills' of Rome. Because it was located between the
       <a href="/wiki/Golden_Horn" title="Golden Horn">
        Golden Horn
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Marmara" title="Sea of Marmara">
        Sea of Marmara
       </a>
       the land area that needed defensive walls was reduced, and this helped it to present an impregnable fortress enclosing magnificent palaces, domes, and towers, the result of the prosperity that was engendered by its being the gateway between two
       <a href="/wiki/Continent" title="Continent">
        continents
       </a>
       (Europe and Asia) and two seas (the Mediterranean and the Black Sea).
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The city was also famed for its architectural masterpieces, such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">
        Greek Orthodox
       </a>
       cathedral of
       <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">
        Hagia Sophia
       </a>
       , which served as the seat of the
       <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Patriarchate_of_Constantinople" title="Ecumenical Patriarchate of Constantinople">
        Ecumenical Patriarchate
       </a>
       , the sacred
       <a href="/wiki/Great_Palace_of_Constantinople" title="Great Palace of Constantinople">
        Imperial Palace
       </a>
       where the Emperors lived, the
       <a href="/wiki/Galata_Tower" title="Galata Tower">
        Galata Tower
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Hippodrome_of_Constantinople" title="Hippodrome of Constantinople">
        Hippodrome
       </a>
       , the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Golden_Gate_(Constantinople)" title="Golden Gate (Constantinople)">
        Golden Gate
       </a>
       of the Land Walls, and the opulent aristocratic palaces lining the arcaded avenues and squares. The
       <a href="/wiki/University_of_Constantinople" title="University of Constantinople">
        University of Constantinople
       </a>
       was founded in the fifth century and contained numerous artistic and literary treasures before it was sacked in 1204 and 1453,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       including its vast
       <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Library_of_Constantinople" title="Imperial Library of Constantinople">
        Imperial Library
       </a>
       which contained the remnants of the
       <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Alexandria" title="Library of Alexandria">
        Library of Alexandria
       </a>
       and had over 100,000 volumes of ancient texts.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Constantinople never truly recovered from the devastation of the Fourth Crusade and the decades of misrule by the Latins. Although the city partially recovered in the early years after the restoration under the
       <a href="/wiki/Palaiologos" title="Palaiologos">
        Palaiologos
       </a>
       dynasty, the advent of the Ottomans and the subsequent loss of the Imperial territories until it became an enclave inside the fledgling Ottoman Empire rendered the city severely depopulated when it fell to the Ottoman Turks,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw28_10-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-mw28-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       whereafter it replaced
       <a href="/wiki/Edirne" title="Edirne">
        Edirne
       </a>
       (Adrianople) as the new capital of the Ottoman Empire.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_11-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Names">
        Names
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Names">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Before_Constantinople">
        Before Constantinople
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Before Constantinople">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       According to
       <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">
        Pliny the Elder
       </a>
       in his
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Natural_History_(Pliny)" title="Natural History (Pliny)">
         Natural History
        </a>
       </i>
       , the first known name of a settlement on the site of Constantinople was
       <i>
        <b>
         Lygos,
        </b>
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       a settlement of likely Thracian origin founded between the 13th and 11th century BC.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The site, according to the founding myth of the city, was abandoned by the time Greek settlers from the city-state of
       <a href="/wiki/Megara" title="Megara">
        Megara
       </a>
       founded
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">
         Byzantium
        </a>
       </i>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">
        Greek
       </a>
       :
       <span lang="el" xml:lang="el">
        Βυζάντιον
       </span>
       ,
       <i>
        Byzántion
       </i>
       ) in around 657 BC,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-roo177_14-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-roo177-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       across from the town of
       <a href="/wiki/Chalcedon" title="Chalcedon">
        Chalcedon
       </a>
       on the Asiatic side of the Bosphorus.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The origins of the name of
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">
         Byzantion
        </a>
       </i>
       , more commonly known by the later Latin
       <i>
        Byzantium,
       </i>
       are not entirely clear, though some suggest it is of Thraco-
       <a href="/wiki/Illyria" title="Illyria">
        Illyrian
       </a>
       origin.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The founding myth of the city has it told that the settlement was named after the leader of the Megarian colonists, Byzas. The later Byzantines of Constantinople themselves would maintain that the city was named in honour of two men, Byzas and Antes, though this was more likely just a play on the word
       <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">
        Byzantion
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:0-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The city was briefly renamed
       <i>
        Augusta Antonina
       </i>
       in the early 3rd century by the Emperor
       <a href="/wiki/Septimius_Severus" title="Septimius Severus">
        Septimius Severus
       </a>
       (193–211), having razed the city to the ground in 196 AD for supporting a
       <a href="/wiki/Pescennius_Niger" title="Pescennius Niger">
        rival contender
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Year_of_the_Five_Emperors" title="Year of the Five Emperors">
        civil war
       </a>
       and rebuilt, in honour of his son Antoninus, the later Emperor
       <a href="/wiki/Caracalla" title="Caracalla">
        Caracalla
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IA_18-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-IA-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The name appears to have been quickly forgotten and abandoned, and the city reverted to Byzantium/Byzantion after either the assassination of Caracalla in 217 or, at the latest, the fall of the
       <a href="/wiki/Severan_dynasty" title="Severan dynasty">
        Severan dynasty
       </a>
       in 235.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Names_of_Constantinople">
        Names of Constantinople
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Names of Constantinople">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Names_of_Constantinople" title="Names of Constantinople">
        Names of Constantinople
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Keystone_Constantine_Forum_Istanbul.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Keystone_Constantine_Forum_Istanbul.JPG/220px-Keystone_Constantine_Forum_Istanbul.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Keystone_Constantine_Forum_Istanbul.JPG/330px-Keystone_Constantine_Forum_Istanbul.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Keystone_Constantine_Forum_Istanbul.JPG/440px-Keystone_Constantine_Forum_Istanbul.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Keystone_Constantine_Forum_Istanbul.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         This huge
         <a href="/wiki/Keystone_(architecture)" title="Keystone (architecture)">
          keystone
         </a>
         found in
         <a href="/wiki/%C3%87emberlita%C5%9F,_Fatih" title="Çemberlitaş, Fatih">
          Çemberlitaş, Fatih
         </a>
         might have belonged to a
         <a href="/wiki/Triumphal_arch" title="Triumphal arch">
          triumphal arch
         </a>
         at the
         <a href="/wiki/Forum_of_Constantine" title="Forum of Constantine">
          Forum of Constantine
         </a>
         ; the forum was built by
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantine_I" title="Constantine I">
          Constantine I
         </a>
         in the quarter of modern-day Çemberlitaş.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Column_of_Constantine,_July_2010.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6048" data-file-width="4032" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Column_of_Constantine%2C_July_2010.jpg/220px-Column_of_Constantine%2C_July_2010.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Column_of_Constantine%2C_July_2010.jpg/330px-Column_of_Constantine%2C_July_2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Column_of_Constantine%2C_July_2010.jpg/440px-Column_of_Constantine%2C_July_2010.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Column_of_Constantine,_July_2010.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The
         <a href="/wiki/Column_of_Constantine" title="Column of Constantine">
          Column of Constantine
         </a>
         , built by
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantine_I" title="Constantine I">
          Constantine I
         </a>
         in 330 AD to commemorate the establishment of Constantinople as the
         <a href="/wiki/New_Rome" title="New Rome">
          new capital
         </a>
         of the
         <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">
          Roman Empire
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Byzantium took on the name of
       <i>
        Konstantinoupolis
       </i>
       ("city of Constantine",
       <i>
        Constantinople
       </i>
       ) after its re-foundation under Roman emperor
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantine_I" title="Constantine I">
        Constantine I
       </a>
       , who transferred the capital of the
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">
        Roman Empire
       </a>
       from
       <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">
        Rome
       </a>
       to Byzantium in 330 AD and designated his new capital officially as
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/New_Rome" title="New Rome">
         Nova Roma
        </a>
       </i>
       (Νέα Ῥώμη) 'New Rome'. During this time, the city was also called 'Second Rome', 'Eastern Rome', and
       <i>
        Roma Constantinopolitana.
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As the city became the sole remaining capital of the Roman Empire after the fall of the West, and its wealth, population, and influence grew, the city also came to have a multitude of nicknames.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       As the largest and wealthiest city in Europe during the 4th–13th centuries and a centre of culture and education of the Mediterranean basin, Constantinople came to be known by prestigious titles such as
       <i>
        Basileuousa
       </i>
       (Queen of Cities) and
       <i>
        Megalopolis
       </i>
       (the Great City) and was, in colloquial speech, commonly referred to as just
       <i>
        Polis
       </i>
       (η πόλη) 'the City' by Constantinopolitans and provincial Byzantines alike.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In the language of other peoples, Constantinople was referred to just as reverently. The medieval Vikings, who had contacts with the empire through their expansion in eastern Europe (
       <a href="/wiki/Varangians" title="Varangians">
        Varangians
       </a>
       ) used the Old Norse name
       <i>
        Miklagarðr
       </i>
       (from
       <i>
        mikill
       </i>
       'big' and
       <i>
        garðr
       </i>
       'city'), and later
       <i>
        Miklagard
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        Miklagarth
       </i>
       . In Arabic, the city was sometimes called
       <i>
        Rūmiyyat al-kubra
       </i>
       (Great City of the Romans) and in Persian as
       <i>
        Takht-e Rum
       </i>
       (Throne of the Romans).
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In East and South Slavic languages, including in
       <a href="/wiki/Kievan_Rus%27" title="Kievan Rus'">
        medieval Russia
       </a>
       , Constantinople was referred to as
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Tsargrad" title="Tsargrad">
         Tsargrad
        </a>
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        Царьград
       </i>
       ) or
       <i>
        Carigrad,
       </i>
       'City of the Caesar (Emperor)', from the Slavonic words
       <i>
        tsar
       </i>
       ('Caesar' or 'King') and
       <i>
        grad
       </i>
       ('city'). This was presumably a
       <a href="/wiki/Calque" title="Calque">
        calque
       </a>
       on a Greek phrase such as Βασιλέως Πόλις
       <i>
        (Vasileos Polis),
       </i>
       'the city of the emperor [king]'.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_names_of_the_city">
        Modern names of the city
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Modern names of the city">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The modern Turkish name for the city,
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C4%B0stanbul" title="İstanbul">
         İstanbul
        </a>
       </i>
       , derives from the
       <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">
        Greek
       </a>
       phrase
       <i>
        eis tin polin
       </i>
       (
       <span lang="el" title="Greek language text" xml:lang="el">
        εἰς τὴν πόλιν
       </span>
       ), meaning "into the city" or "to the city".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This name was used in
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">
        Turkish
       </a>
       alongside
       <i>
        Kostantiniyye
       </i>
       , the more formal adaptation of the original
       <i>
        Constantinople
       </i>
       , during the period of
       <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">
        Ottoman
       </a>
       rule, while western languages mostly continued to refer to the city as Constantinople until the early 20th century. In 1928,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turkish_Language_Commission" title="Turkish Language Commission">
        the Turkish alphabet was changed
       </a>
       from Arabic script to Latin script. After that, as part of the 1920s
       <a href="/wiki/Turkification" title="Turkification">
        Turkification
       </a>
       movement, Turkey started to urge other countries to use
       <a href="/wiki/Geographical_name_changes_in_Turkey" title="Geographical name changes in Turkey">
        Turkish names for Turkish cities
       </a>
       , instead of other transliterations to Latin script that had been used in the Ottoman times.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shawn_23-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-shawn-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In time the city came to be known as Istanbul and its variations in most world languages.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The name "Constantinople" is still used by members of the
       <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">
        Eastern Orthodox Church
       </a>
       in the title of one of their most important leaders, the Orthodox
       <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Patriarch_of_Constantinople" title="Ecumenical Patriarch of Constantinople">
        patriarch
       </a>
       based in the city, referred to as "His Most Divine All-Holiness the Archbishop of Constantinople New Rome and Ecumenical Patriarch." In Greece today, the city is still called Konstantinoúpolis/Konstantinoúpoli (Κωνσταντινούπολη/Κωνσταντινούπολις) or simply just "the City" (Η Πόλη / Η Πόλις).
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Byzantium_and_earlier_settlements">
        Byzantium and earlier settlements
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Byzantium and earlier settlements">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">
        Byzantium
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Constantinople was founded by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Emperor" title="Roman Emperor">
        Roman Emperor
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantine_I" title="Constantine I">
        Constantine I
       </a>
       (272–337 AD) in 324
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODB_5-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ODB-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       on the site of an already-existing city,
       <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">
        Byzantium
       </a>
       , which was settled in the early days of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_colonies" title="Greek colonies">
        Greek colonial expansion
       </a>
       , in around 657 BC, by colonists of the city-state of
       <a href="/wiki/Megara" title="Megara">
        Megara
       </a>
       . This is the first major settlement that would develop on the site of later Constantinople, but the first known settlements was that of
       <i>
        Lygos
       </i>
       , referred to in Pliny's Natural Histories,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Apart from this, little is known about this initial settlement. The site, according to the founding myth of the city, was abandoned by the time Greek settlers from the city-state of Megara founded Byzantium (Greek: Βυζάντιον, Byzántion) in around 657 BC,[14] across from the town of Chalcedon on the Asiatic side of the Bosphorus.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The city maintained independence as a city-state until it was annexed by
       <a href="/wiki/Darius_I" title="Darius I">
        Darius I
       </a>
       in 512 BC into the
       <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">
        Persian Empire
       </a>
       , who saw the site as the optimal location to construct a
       <a href="/wiki/Pontoon_bridge" title="Pontoon bridge">
        pontoon bridge
       </a>
       crossing into Europe as Byzantium was situated at the narrowest point in the Bosphorus strait. Persian rule lasted until 478 BC when as part of the Greek counterattack to the
       <a href="/wiki/Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" title="Second Persian invasion of Greece">
        Second Persian Invasion of Greece
       </a>
       , a Greek army led by the Spartan general
       <a href="/wiki/Pausanias_(general)" title="Pausanias (general)">
        Pausanias
       </a>
       captured the city which remained an independent, yet subordinate, city under the Athenians, and later to the Spartans after 411 BC.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A farsighted treaty with the emergent power of Rome in c.150 BC which stipulated tribute in exchange for independent status allowed it to enter Roman rule unscathed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This treaty would pay dividends retrospectively as Byzantium would maintain this independent status, and prosper under peace and stability in the
       <a href="/wiki/Pax_Romana" title="Pax Romana">
        Pax Romana
       </a>
       , for nearly three centuries until the late 2nd century AD.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Byzantium was never a major influential city-state like that of Athens, Corinth, and Sparta, but the city enjoyed relative peace and steady growth as a prosperous trading city lent by its remarkable position. The site lay astride the land route from
       <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">
        Europe
       </a>
       to
       <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">
        Asia
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_Straits" title="Turkish Straits">
        seaway
       </a>
       from the
       <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">
        Black Sea
       </a>
       to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediterranean" title="Mediterranean">
        Mediterranean
       </a>
       , and had in the
       <a href="/wiki/Golden_Horn" title="Golden Horn">
        Golden Horn
       </a>
       an excellent and spacious harbour. Already then, in Greek and early Roman times, Byzantium was famous for its strategic geographic position that made it difficult to besiege and capture, and its position at the crossroads of the Asiatic-European trade route over land and as the gateway between the Mediterranean and Black Seas made it too valuable a settlement to abandon, as Emperor
       <a href="/wiki/Septimius_Severus" title="Septimius Severus">
        Septimius Severus
       </a>
       later realized when he razed the city to the ground for supporting
       <a href="/wiki/Pescennius_Niger" title="Pescennius Niger">
        Pescennius Niger
       </a>
       's
       <a href="/wiki/Year_of_the_Five_Emperors" title="Year of the Five Emperors">
        claimancy
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was a move greatly criticized by the contemporary consul and historian
       <a href="/wiki/Cassius_Dio" title="Cassius Dio">
        Cassius Dio
       </a>
       who said that Severus had destroyed "a strong Roman outpost and a base of operations against the barbarians from Pontus and Asia".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       He would later rebuild Byzantium towards the end of his reign, in which it would be briefly renamed
       <i>
        Augusta Antonina
       </i>
       , fortifying it with a new city wall in his name, the Severan Wall.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="324.E2.80.93337:_Foundation_of_Constantinople">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="324–337:_Foundation_of_Constantinople">
        324–337: Foundation of Constantinople
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 324–337: Foundation of Constantinople">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Constantine_I_Hagia_Sophia.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="702" data-file-width="688" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Constantine_I_Hagia_Sophia.jpg/220px-Constantine_I_Hagia_Sophia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Constantine_I_Hagia_Sophia.jpg/330px-Constantine_I_Hagia_Sophia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Constantine_I_Hagia_Sophia.jpg/440px-Constantine_I_Hagia_Sophia.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Constantine_I_Hagia_Sophia.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Emperor
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantine_I" title="Constantine I">
          Constantine I
         </a>
         presents a representation of the city of Constantinople as tribute to an enthroned Mary and Christ Child in this church mosaic.
         <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">
          Hagia Sophia
         </a>
         , c. 1000.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Urbs_Roma,_commemorative_coin_of_Constantinople.jpg">
         <img alt="Commemorative Ancient Coin of Constantinople" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="423" data-file-width="912" height="102" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Urbs_Roma%2C_commemorative_coin_of_Constantinople.jpg/220px-Urbs_Roma%2C_commemorative_coin_of_Constantinople.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Urbs_Roma%2C_commemorative_coin_of_Constantinople.jpg/330px-Urbs_Roma%2C_commemorative_coin_of_Constantinople.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Urbs_Roma%2C_commemorative_coin_of_Constantinople.jpg/440px-Urbs_Roma%2C_commemorative_coin_of_Constantinople.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Urbs_Roma,_commemorative_coin_of_Constantinople.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Another coin struck by Constantine I in 330–333 AD to commemorate the foundation of Constantinople and to also reaffirm Rome as the traditional centre of the Roman Empire.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Constantinopolis_coin.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="394" data-file-width="798" height="109" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Constantinopolis_coin.jpg/220px-Constantinopolis_coin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Constantinopolis_coin.jpg/330px-Constantinopolis_coin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Constantinopolis_coin.jpg/440px-Constantinopolis_coin.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Constantinopolis_coin.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Coin" title="Coin">
          Coin
         </a>
         struck by Constantine I to commemorate the founding of Constantinople.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Constantine had altogether more colourful plans. Having restored the unity of the Empire, and, being in the course of major governmental reforms as well as of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantine_I_and_Christianity" title="Constantine I and Christianity">
        sponsoring the consolidation of the Christian church
       </a>
       , he was well aware that Rome was an unsatisfactory capital. Rome was too far from the frontiers, and hence from the armies and the imperial courts, and it offered an undesirable playground for disaffected politicians. Yet it had been the capital of the state for over a thousand years, and it might have seemed unthinkable to suggest that the capital be moved to a different location. Nevertheless, Constantine identified the site of Byzantium as the right place: a place where an emperor could sit, readily defended, with easy access to the
       <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">
        Danube
       </a>
       or the
       <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">
        Euphrates
       </a>
       frontiers, his court supplied from the rich gardens and sophisticated workshops of Roman Asia, his treasuries filled by the wealthiest provinces of the Empire.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Constantinople was built over 6 years, and consecrated on 11 May 330.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODB_5-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-ODB-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Constantine divided the expanded city, like Rome, into 14 regions, and ornamented it with public works worthy of an imperial metropolis.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Yet, at first, Constantine's new Rome did not have all the dignities of old Rome. It possessed a
       <a href="/wiki/Proconsul" title="Proconsul">
        proconsul
       </a>
       , rather than an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urban_prefect" title="Urban prefect">
        urban prefect
       </a>
       . It had no
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Praetors" title="Praetors">
        praetors
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tribunes" title="Tribunes">
        tribunes
       </a>
       , or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quaestors" title="Quaestors">
        quaestors
       </a>
       . Although it did have senators, they held the title
       <i>
        clarus
       </i>
       , not
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clarissimus" title="Clarissimus">
         clarissimus
        </a>
       </i>
       , like those of Rome. It also lacked the panoply of other administrative offices regulating the food supply, police, statues, temples, sewers, aqueducts, or other public works. The new programme of building was carried out in great haste: columns, marbles, doors, and tiles were taken wholesale from the temples of the empire and moved to the new city. In similar fashion, many of the greatest works of Greek and Roman art were soon to be seen in its squares and streets. The emperor stimulated private building by promising householders gifts of land from the imperial estates in
       <a href="/wiki/Diocese_of_Asia" title="Diocese of Asia">
        Asiana
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Diocese_of_Pontus" title="Diocese of Pontus">
        Pontica
       </a>
       and on 18 May 332 he announced that, as in Rome, free distributions of food would be made to the citizens. At the time, the amount is said to have been 80,000 rations a day, doled out from 117 distribution points around the city.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Constantine laid out a new square at the centre of old Byzantium, naming it the
       <a href="/wiki/Augustaion" title="Augustaion">
        Augustaeum
       </a>
       . The new senate-house (or Curia) was housed in a basilica on the east side. On the south side of the great square was erected the
       <a href="/wiki/Great_Palace_of_Constantinople" title="Great Palace of Constantinople">
        Great Palace
       </a>
       of the Emperor with its imposing entrance, the
       <a href="/wiki/Chalke" title="Chalke">
        Chalke
       </a>
       , and its ceremonial suite known as the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daphne_Palace" title="Daphne Palace">
        Palace of Daphne
       </a>
       . Nearby was the vast
       <a href="/wiki/Hippodrome_of_Constantinople" title="Hippodrome of Constantinople">
        Hippodrome
       </a>
       for chariot-races, seating over 80,000 spectators, and the famed
       <a href="/wiki/Baths_of_Zeuxippus" title="Baths of Zeuxippus">
        Baths of Zeuxippus
       </a>
       . At the western entrance to the Augustaeum was the
       <a href="/wiki/Milion" title="Milion">
        Milion
       </a>
       , a vaulted monument from which distances were measured across the Eastern Roman Empire.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       From the Augustaeum led a great street, the
       <a href="/wiki/Mese_(Constantinople)" title="Mese (Constantinople)">
        Mese
       </a>
       (Greek: Μέση [Οδός] lit. "Middle [Street]"), lined with colonnades. As it descended the First Hill of the city and climbed the Second Hill, it passed on the left the
       <a href="/wiki/Praetorium" title="Praetorium">
        Praetorium
       </a>
       or law-court. Then it passed through the oval
       <a href="/wiki/Forum_of_Constantine" title="Forum of Constantine">
        Forum of Constantine
       </a>
       where there was a second Senate-house and a
       <a href="/wiki/Column_of_Constantine" title="Column of Constantine">
        high column
       </a>
       with a statue of Constantine himself in the guise of
       <a href="/wiki/Helios" title="Helios">
        Helios
       </a>
       , crowned with a halo of seven rays and looking toward the rising sun. From there, the Mese passed on and through the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forum_Tauri" title="Forum Tauri">
        Forum Tauri
       </a>
       and then the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forum_Bovis" title="Forum Bovis">
        Forum Bovis
       </a>
       , and finally up the Seventh Hill (or Xerolophus) and through to the Golden Gate in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wall_of_Constantine_(Constantinople)" title="Wall of Constantine (Constantinople)">
        Constantinian Wall
       </a>
       . After the construction of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theodosian_Walls" title="Theodosian Walls">
        Theodosian Walls
       </a>
       in the early 5th century, it was extended to the new
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Golden_Gate_(Constantinople)" title="Golden Gate (Constantinople)">
        Golden Gate
       </a>
       , reaching a total length of seven
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_mile" title="Roman mile">
        Roman miles
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="337.E2.80.93529:_Constantinople_during_the_Barbarian_Invasions_and_the_fall_of_the_West">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="337–529:_Constantinople_during_the_Barbarian_Invasions_and_the_fall_of_the_West">
        337–529: Constantinople during the Barbarian Invasions and the fall of the West
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: 337–529: Constantinople during the Barbarian Invasions and the fall of the West">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Theodosius_colum,_Istanbul.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="530" data-file-width="425" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Theodosius_colum%2C_Istanbul.jpg/120px-Theodosius_colum%2C_Istanbul.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Theodosius_colum%2C_Istanbul.jpg/180px-Theodosius_colum%2C_Istanbul.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Theodosius_colum%2C_Istanbul.jpg/240px-Theodosius_colum%2C_Istanbul.jpg 2x" width="120"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Theodosius_colum,_Istanbul.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_I" title="Theodosius I">
          Theodosius I
         </a>
         was the last
         <a href="/wiki/Roman_emperor" title="Roman emperor">
          Roman emperor
         </a>
         who ruled over an undivided empire (detail from the Obelisk at the
         <a href="/wiki/Hippodrome_of_Constantinople" title="Hippodrome of Constantinople">
          Hippodrome of Constantinople
         </a>
         ).
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The importance of Constantinople increased, but it was gradual. From the death of Constantine in 337 to the accession of Theodosius I, emperors had been resident only in the years 337-8, 347–51, 358–61, 368–69. Its status as a capital was recognized by the appointment of the first known Urban Prefect of the City Honoratus, who held office from 11 December 359 until 361. The urban prefects had concurrent jurisdiction over three provinces each in the adjacent dioceses of Thrace (in which the city was located), Pontus and Asia comparable to the 100-mile extraordinary jurisdiction of the prefect of Rome. The emperor
       <a href="/wiki/Valens" title="Valens">
        Valens
       </a>
       , who hated the city and spent only one year there, nevertheless built the Palace of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hebdomon" title="Hebdomon">
        Hebdomon
       </a>
       on the shore of the
       <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Marmara" title="Sea of Marmara">
        Propontis
       </a>
       near the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Golden_Gate_(Constantinople)" title="Golden Gate (Constantinople)">
        Golden Gate
       </a>
       , probably for use when reviewing troops. All the emperors up to
       <a href="/wiki/Zeno_(emperor)" title="Zeno (emperor)">
        Zeno
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Basiliscus" title="Basiliscus">
        Basiliscus
       </a>
       were crowned and acclaimed at the Hebdomon.
       <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_I" title="Theodosius I">
        Theodosius I
       </a>
       founded the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Studion" title="Studion">
        Church of John the Baptist
       </a>
       to house the skull of the saint (today preserved at the
       <a href="/wiki/Topkap%C4%B1_Palace" title="Topkapı Palace">
        Topkapı Palace
       </a>
       ), put up a memorial pillar to himself in the Forum of Taurus, and turned the ruined temple of
       <a href="/wiki/Aphrodite" title="Aphrodite">
        Aphrodite
       </a>
       into a coach house for the
       <a href="/wiki/Praetorian_prefecture_of_the_East" title="Praetorian prefecture of the East">
        Praetorian Prefect
       </a>
       ;
       <a href="/wiki/Arcadius" title="Arcadius">
        Arcadius
       </a>
       built a new forum named after himself on the Mese, near the walls of Constantine.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       After the shock of the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Adrianople" title="Battle of Adrianople">
        Battle of Adrianople
       </a>
       in 378, in which the emperor
       <a href="/wiki/Valens" title="Valens">
        Valens
       </a>
       with the flower of the Roman armies was destroyed by the
       <a href="/wiki/Visigoths" title="Visigoths">
        Visigoths
       </a>
       within a few days' march, the city looked to its defences, and in 413–414
       <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_II" title="Theodosius II">
        Theodosius II
       </a>
       built the 18-metre (60-foot)-tall
       <a href="/wiki/Walls_of_Constantinople" title="Walls of Constantinople">
        triple-wall fortifications
       </a>
       , which were not to be breached until the coming of gunpowder. Theodosius also founded a
       <a href="/wiki/University_of_Constantinople" title="University of Constantinople">
        University
       </a>
       near the Forum of Taurus, on 27 February 425.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Uldin" title="Uldin">
        Uldin
       </a>
       , a prince of the
       <a href="/wiki/Huns" title="Huns">
        Huns
       </a>
       , appeared on the Danube about this time and advanced into Thrace, but he was deserted by many of his followers, who joined with the Romans in driving their king back north of the river. Subsequent to this, new walls were built to defend the city and the fleet on the Danube improved.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       After the
       <a href="/wiki/Barbarian" title="Barbarian">
        barbarians
       </a>
       overran the Western Roman Empire, Constantinople became the indisputable capital city of the Roman Empire. Emperors were no longer peripatetic between various court capitals and palaces. They remained in their palace in the Great City and sent generals to command their armies. The wealth of the eastern Mediterranean and western Asia flowed into Constantinople.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="527.E2.80.93565:_Constantinople_in_the_Age_of_Justinian">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="527–565:_Constantinople_in_the_Age_of_Justinian">
        527–565: Constantinople in the Age of Justinian
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: 527–565: Constantinople in the Age of Justinian">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Constantinople_(1422)_by_Florentine_cartographer_Cristoforo_Buondelmonte.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="746" data-file-width="592" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Map_of_Constantinople_%281422%29_by_Florentine_cartographer_Cristoforo_Buondelmonte.jpg/120px-Map_of_Constantinople_%281422%29_by_Florentine_cartographer_Cristoforo_Buondelmonte.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Map_of_Constantinople_%281422%29_by_Florentine_cartographer_Cristoforo_Buondelmonte.jpg/180px-Map_of_Constantinople_%281422%29_by_Florentine_cartographer_Cristoforo_Buondelmonte.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Map_of_Constantinople_%281422%29_by_Florentine_cartographer_Cristoforo_Buondelmonte.jpg/240px-Map_of_Constantinople_%281422%29_by_Florentine_cartographer_Cristoforo_Buondelmonte.jpg 2x" width="120"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Constantinople_(1422)_by_Florentine_cartographer_Cristoforo_Buondelmonte.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Map of Constantinople (1422) by Florentine cartographer
         <a href="/wiki/Cristoforo_Buondelmonti" title="Cristoforo Buondelmonti">
          Cristoforo Buondelmonti
         </a>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
          <a href="#cite_note-37">
           [37]
          </a>
         </sup>
         is the oldest surviving map of the city, and the only one that predates the Turkish conquest of the city in 1453.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hagia_Sophia_Mars_2013.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3681" data-file-width="5514" height="80" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Hagia_Sophia_Mars_2013.jpg/120px-Hagia_Sophia_Mars_2013.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Hagia_Sophia_Mars_2013.jpg/180px-Hagia_Sophia_Mars_2013.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Hagia_Sophia_Mars_2013.jpg/240px-Hagia_Sophia_Mars_2013.jpg 2x" width="120"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hagia_Sophia_Mars_2013.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The current
         <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">
          Hagia Sophia
         </a>
         was commissioned by Emperor
         <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">
          Justinian I
         </a>
         after the previous one was destroyed in the
         <a href="/wiki/Nika_riots" title="Nika riots">
          Nika riots
         </a>
         of 532. It was converted into a mosque in 1453 when the Ottoman Empire commenced and became a museum in 1935.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The emperor
       <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">
        Justinian I
       </a>
       (527–565) was known for his successes in war, for his legal reforms and for his public works. It was from Constantinople that his expedition for the reconquest of the former Diocese of Africa set sail on or about 21 June 533. Before their departure, the ship of the commander
       <a href="/wiki/Belisarius" title="Belisarius">
        Belisarius
       </a>
       was anchored in front of the Imperial palace, and the Patriarch offered prayers for the success of the enterprise. After the victory, in 534, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herod%27s_Temple" title="Herod's Temple">
        Temple treasure of Jerusalem
       </a>
       , looted by the Romans in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siege_of_Jerusalem_(70)" title="Siege of Jerusalem (70)">
        70 AD
       </a>
       and taken to
       <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">
        Carthage
       </a>
       by the
       <a href="/wiki/Vandals" title="Vandals">
        Vandals
       </a>
       after their sack of Rome in 455, was brought to Constantinople and deposited for a time, perhaps in the
       <a href="/wiki/Church_of_St._Polyeuctus" title="Church of St. Polyeuctus">
        Church of St. Polyeuctus
       </a>
       , before being returned to
       <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem_in_Christianity" title="Jerusalem in Christianity">
        Jerusalem
       </a>
       in either the
       <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_Holy_Sepulchre" title="Church of the Holy Sepulchre">
        Church of the Resurrection
       </a>
       or the New Church.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Chariot-racing had been important in Rome for centuries. In Constantinople, the hippodrome became over time increasingly a place of political significance. It was where (as a shadow of the popular elections of old Rome) the people by acclamation showed their approval of a new emperor, and also where they openly criticized the government, or clamoured for the removal of unpopular ministers. In the time of Justinian, public order in Constantinople became a critical political issue.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Throughout the late Roman and early Byzantine periods, Christianity was resolving fundamental questions of identity, and the dispute between the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chalcedonian_Orthodoxy" title="Chalcedonian Orthodoxy">
        orthodox
       </a>
       and the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monophysites" title="Monophysites">
        monophysites
       </a>
       became the cause of serious disorder, expressed through allegiance to the horse-racing parties of the Blues and the Greens. The partisans of the Blues and the Greens were said
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       to affect untrimmed facial hair, head hair shaved at the front and grown long at the back, and wide-sleeved tunics tight at the wrist; and to form gangs to engage in night-time muggings and street violence. At last these disorders took the form of a major rebellion of 532, known as the
       <a href="/wiki/Nika_riots" title="Nika riots">
        "Nika" riots
       </a>
       (from the battle-cry of "Victory!" of those involved).
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Fires started by the Nika rioters consumed Constantine's basilica of Hagia Sophia (Holy Wisdom), the city's principal church, which lay to the north of the Augustaeum. Justinian commissioned
       <a href="/wiki/Anthemius_of_Tralles" title="Anthemius of Tralles">
        Anthemius of Tralles
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Isidore_of_Miletus" title="Isidore of Miletus">
        Isidore of Miletus
       </a>
       to replace it with a new and incomparable
       <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">
        Hagia Sophia
       </a>
       . This was the great cathedral of the Orthodox Church, whose dome was said to be held aloft by God alone, and which was directly connected to the palace so that the imperial family could attend services without passing through the streets.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The dedication took place on 26 December 537 in the presence of the emperor, who exclaimed, "O
       <a href="/wiki/Solomon%27s_Temple" title="Solomon's Temple">
        Solomon
       </a>
       , I have outdone thee!"
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Hagia Sophia was served by 600 people including 80 priests, and cost 20,000 pounds of gold to build.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cost_42-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-cost-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Justinian also had Anthemius and Isidore demolish and replace the original Church of the Holy Apostles built by Constantine with a
       <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_Holy_Apostles" title="Church of the Holy Apostles">
        new church
       </a>
       under the same dedication. This was designed in the form of an equal-armed cross with five domes, and ornamented with beautiful mosaics. This church was to remain the burial place of the Emperors from Constantine himself until the 11th century. When the city fell to the Turks in 1453, the church was demolished to make room for the tomb of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mehmet_II" title="Mehmet II">
        Mehmet II
       </a>
       the Conqueror. Justinian was also concerned with other aspects of the city's built environment, legislating against the abuse of laws prohibiting building within 100 feet (30 m) of the sea front, in order to protect the view.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       During Justinian I's reign, the city's population reached about 500,000 people.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, the social fabric of Constantinople was also damaged by the onset of the
       <a href="/wiki/Plague_of_Justinian" title="Plague of Justinian">
        Plague of Justinian
       </a>
       between 541–542 AD. It killed perhaps 40% of the city's inhabitants.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Walls_of_Constantinople.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1920" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Walls_of_Constantinople.JPG/220px-Walls_of_Constantinople.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Walls_of_Constantinople.JPG/330px-Walls_of_Constantinople.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Walls_of_Constantinople.JPG/440px-Walls_of_Constantinople.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Walls_of_Constantinople.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Restored section of the fortifications that protected Constantinople during the
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval_period" title="Medieval period">
          medieval period
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span id="Survival.2C_565.E2.80.93717:_Constantinople_during_the_Byzantine_Dark_Ages">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Survival,_565–717:_Constantinople_during_the_Byzantine_Dark_Ages">
        Survival, 565–717: Constantinople during the Byzantine Dark Ages
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Survival, 565–717: Constantinople during the Byzantine Dark Ages">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In the early 7th century, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avars_(Carpathians)" title="Avars (Carpathians)">
        Avars
       </a>
       and later the
       <a href="/wiki/Bulgars" title="Bulgars">
        Bulgars
       </a>
       overwhelmed much of the
       <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">
        Balkans
       </a>
       , threatening Constantinople with attack from the west. Simultaneously, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">
        Persian
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sassanid" title="Sassanid">
        Sassanids
       </a>
       overwhelmed the Prefecture of the East and penetrated deep into
       <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">
        Anatolia
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Heraclius" title="Heraclius">
        Heraclius
       </a>
       , son of the
       <a href="/wiki/Exarch" title="Exarch">
        exarch
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">
        Africa
       </a>
       , set sail for the city and assumed the purple. He found the military situation so dire that he is said to have contemplated withdrawing the imperial capital to Carthage, but relented after the people of Constantinople begged him to stay. The citizens lost their right to free grain in 618 when Heraclius realised that the city could no longer be supplied from Egypt as a result of the Persian wars: the population fell substantially as a result.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       While the city withstood a
       <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Constantinople_(626)" title="Siege of Constantinople (626)">
        siege
       </a>
       by the Sassanids and Avars in 626, Heraclius campaigned deep into Persian territory and briefly restored the
       <i>
        status quo
       </i>
       in 628, when the Persians surrendered all their conquests. However, further sieges followed the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine%E2%80%93Arab_Wars" title="Byzantine–Arab Wars">
        Arab conquests
       </a>
       , first from
       <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Constantinople_(674%E2%80%93678)" title="Siege of Constantinople (674–678)">
        674 to 678
       </a>
       and then in
       <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Constantinople_(717%E2%80%93718)" title="Siege of Constantinople (717–718)">
        717 to 718
       </a>
       . The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theodosian_Walls" title="Theodosian Walls">
        Theodosian Walls
       </a>
       kept the city impregnable from the land, while a newly discovered incendiary substance known as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_Fire" title="Greek Fire">
        Greek Fire
       </a>
       allowed the
       <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_navy" title="Byzantine navy">
        Byzantine navy
       </a>
       to destroy the Arab fleets and keep the city supplied. In the second siege, the second ruler of
       <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">
        Bulgaria
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Tervel_of_Bulgaria" title="Tervel of Bulgaria">
        Khan Tervel
       </a>
       , rendered decisive help. He was called
       <i>
        Saviour of Europe
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="717.E2.80.931025:_Constantinople_during_the_Macedonian_Renaissance">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="717–1025:_Constantinople_during_the_Macedonian_Renaissance">
        717–1025: Constantinople during the Macedonian Renaissance
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Constantinople&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: 717–1025: Constantinople during the Macedonian Renaissance">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Imperial_Gate_mosaic_in_Hagia_Sophia.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="3072" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Imperial_Gate_mosaic_in_Hagia_Sophia.jpg/220px-Imperial_Gate_mosaic_in_Hagia_Sophia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Imperial_Gate_mosaic_in_Hagia_Sophia.jpg/330px-Imperial_Gate_mosaic_in_Hagia_Sophia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Imperial_Gate_mosaic_in_Hagia_Sophia.jpg/440px-Imperial_Gate_mosaic_in_Hagia_Sophia.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Imperial_Gate_mosaic_in_Hagia_Sophia.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emperor_Leo_VI" title="Emperor Leo VI">
          Emperor Leo VI
         </a>
         (886–912) adoring
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesus_Christ" title="Jesus Christ">
          Jesus Christ
         </a>
         .
         <a href="/wiki/Mosaic" title="Mosaic">
          Mosaic
         </a>
         above the Imperial Gate in the
         <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">
          Hagia Sophia
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In the 730s
       <a href="/wiki/Leo_III_the_Isaurian" title="Leo III the Isaurian">
        Leo III
       </a>
       carried out extensive repairs of the Theodosian walls, which had been damaged by frequent and violent attacks; this work was financed by a special tax on all the subjects of the Empire.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Theodora, widow of the Emperor
       <a href="/wiki/Theophilos_(emperor)" title="Theophilos (emperor)">
        Theophilus
       </a>
       (died 842), acted as regent during the minority of her son
       <a href="/wiki/Michael_III" title="Michael III">
        Michael III
       </a>
       , who was said to have been introduced to dissolute habits by her brother Bardas. When Michael assumed power in 856, he became known for excessive drunkenness, appeared in the hippodrome as a charioteer and burlesqued the religious processions of the clergy. He removed Theodora from the Great Palace to the Carian Palace and later to the
       <a href="/wiki/Sancaktar_Hayrettin_Mosque" title="Sancaktar Hayrettin Mosque">
        monastery of Gastria
       </a>
       , but, after the death of Bardas, she was released to live in the palace of St Mamas; she also had a rural residence at the Anthemian Palace, where Michael was assassinated in 867.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 860, an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rus%27%E2%80%93Byzantine_War_(860)" title="Rus'–Byzantine War (860)">
        attack
       </a>
       was made on the city by a new principality set up a few years earlier at
       <a href="/wiki/Kiev" title="Kiev">
        Kiev
       </a>
       by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Askold_and_Dir" title="Askold and Dir">
        Askold and Dir
       </a>
       , two
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Varangian" title="Varangian">
        Varangian
       </a>
       chiefs: Two hundred small vessels passed through the Bosporus and plundered the monasteries and other properties on the suburban
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prince%27s_Islands" title="Prince's Islands">
        Prince's Islands
       </a>
       .
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Niketas_Oryphas" title="Niketas Oryphas">
        Oryphas
       </a>
